  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer7584387833081169457">The Education of Little Tree tells of a boy orphaned very young, who is adopted by his Cherokee grandmother and half-Cherokee grandfather in the Appalachian mountains of Tennessee during the Great Depression. Little Tree as his grandparents call him is shown how to hunt and survive in the mountains, to respect nature in the Cherokee Way, taking only what is needed, leaving</span>
  <span id="freeText7584387833081169457" style="display:none">The Education of Little Tree tells of a boy orphaned very young, who is adopted by his Cherokee grandmother and half-Cherokee grandfather in the Appalachian mountains of Tennessee during the Great Depression. Little Tree as his grandparents call him is shown how to hunt and survive in the mountains, to respect nature in the Cherokee Way, taking only what is needed, leaving the rest for nature to run its course. Little Tree also learns the often callous ways of white businessmen and tax collectors, and how Granpa, in hilarious vignettes, scares them away from his illegal attempts to enter the cash economy. Granma teaches Little Tree the joys of reading and education. But when Little Tree is taken away by whites for schooling, we learn of the cruelty meted out to Indian children in an attempt to assimilate them and of Little Tree's perception of the Anglo world and how it differs from the Cherokee Way. A classic of its era, and an enduring book for all ages, The Education of Little Tree has now been redesigned for this twenty-fifth anniversary edition.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7584387833081169457" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/46928617-genova-vader">Genova Vader</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I thought it was a memoir when I read it for a children's lit class in college. it became my favorite book and then most hated author, after the slow-<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I thought it was a memoir when I read it for a children's lit class in college. it became my favorite book and then most hated author, after the slow-burn of realizing the racism was supported, not suffered. Should we judge a book for its beauty, separating it from its author? It is only in NOT reconciling the two that I can say that I love this book and that it had a profound impact on me, especially when it comes to Christmas trees. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/12850803-joan-broadfield"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1347714541i/12850803._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/12850803-joan-broadfield">Joan Broadfield</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Hmmm... As I read it, I became suspicious. It is possible that some will not see an underlying perspective but I'd want some folks of the community he<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Hmmm... As I read it, I became suspicious. It is possible that some will not see an underlying perspective but I'd want some folks of the community he was 'imagining' before I'd recommend it as a 'good read' that has 'no hint' of baggage. <br /><br />The 'mental models' we absorb about those different from us can put spins of positive or negative aspects that do not reflect the reality of experience, but simply imagination. If someone reads this as a book about a native american, they are not getting that perspective in fact.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1861230" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1861230" />
    <a title="J.G. Keely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely"><img alt="J.G. Keely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274333636p2/84023.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1861230?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.G. Keely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.G. Keely" href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely">J.G. Keely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780826308795" href="/book/show/829675.The_Education_of_Little_Tree">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=contemporary-fiction">contemporary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=america">america</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1861230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8781460679610899774">The closest this book gets to touching nature is the sweet sappiness of the story. Though the author put the story forward as true, he was not actually a Native, but a <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asa_Earl_Carter' rel='nofollow noopener'>racist con-man</a> who fought to keep segregation and was a member of the KKK. <br/><br/>But this revelation shouldn't be that surprising, since the book is hardly insightful or sensitive in its views. Carter's characters are old, romanticized cliches of the colonial <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noble_savage' rel='nofollow noopener'>'Noble Savage'</a>--poor Indians beset by the white man's greed trying to eke a pe</span>
  <span id="freeText8781460679610899774" style="display:none">The closest this book gets to touching nature is the sweet sappiness of the story. Though the author put the story forward as true, he was not actually a Native, but a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asa_Earl_Carter" rel="nofollow noopener">racist con-man</a> who fought to keep segregation and was a member of the KKK. <br /><br />But this revelation shouldn't be that surprising, since the book is hardly insightful or sensitive in its views. Carter's characters are old, romanticized cliches of the colonial <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noble_savage" rel="nofollow noopener">'Noble Savage'</a>--poor Indians beset by the white man's greed trying to eke a peaceful and natural existence out in the wild of nature. It should remind us all that an overly rosy view can be just as racist and condescending as a negative one.<br /><br />Carter is just another in a long line of people who tried to make themselves more mysterious and interesting by making up a distant Native ancestor and then claiming it gives them some kind of spiritual and moral superiority. I guess I should mention here that it's overtly racist to imagine that a fully-formed culture can be propagated through blood, as if Native peoples were magic elves.<br /><br />But people like to individualize themselves, and if that means they have to create a culture from whole cloth to belong to, that isn't going to stop them, whether it's someone bringing up their '1/16th Cherokee blood' or a Wiccan who doesn't realize they're following <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tarot" rel="nofollow noopener">Christian mysticism</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Witch-cult_hypothesis" rel="nofollow noopener">conspiracy theories</a>, and some stuff that was made up by delusionals and con-men.<br /><br />And if that wasn't enough to tip us off, there's also a lengthy sambo slapstick scene almost as insulting to blacks as Martin Lawrence in a fatsuit. It just goes to show that it's easy to fool people with over-the-top cliches and over-romanticized characters. Even Oprah was taken in, featuring this book in her reading club--but perhaps it shouldn't surprise us that one purveyor of ill-informed saccharine melodrama should be taken in by another.<br /><br />In the end, we get a sort of literary version of the blackface minstrel show, depicting Native life with a quaint nostalgia that has nothing to do with the real experience of Natives or their history. Instead, everything is boiled down into a simple little story--almost a fable--of how the colonial mindset would prefer to see Natives: as fundamentally separate in vague, mystical ways. <br /><br />They are so oversimplified (as heroes or villains) that they no longer resemble real people; instead, they are reduced to a subspecies of man defined by a set of universally shared traits. Their identity is primarily communal, primarily traditional, incapable of change, learning, or individuality.<br /><br />It's hard for me to think of a more pointed definition or racism than 'assuming that a group of people, similar in appearance and ancestry, all share a series of invariable traits which make them fundamentally and inescapably different from every other individual and people group'.<br /><br />Like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/22703379" rel="nofollow noopener">'The Kite Runner'</a>, this is just another book that assuages white guilt by making white readers feel that, in just picking up a book, they have become worldly, understanding, and compassionate--despite the fact that neither book really reveals the culture it set out to depict, and could not provide any real insight to anyone who was in the least familiar with how those cultures actually work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8781460679610899774" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1651697613" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1651697613" />
    <a title="Diane S ☔" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4159922-diane-s"><img alt="Diane S ☔" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598402138p2/4159922.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1651697613?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane S ☔" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane S ☔" href="/user/show/4159922-diane-s">Diane S ☔</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1651697613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10794151599111292029">Within the first three pages I fell in love with our four year old narrator, whose grandfather called Little Tree. His relationships with his grandparents reminded me so much of mine, it was hard not to identify with that even though his Cherokee culture was of course different. Still, the love, the knowledge, the ways shown to live were in many ways, different but the same. So Little Tree learns from his grandparents the way of the Indian and how to navigate the world of the white man. Loved wa</span>
  <span id="freeText10794151599111292029" style="display:none">Within the first three pages I fell in love with our four year old narrator, whose grandfather called Little Tree. His relationships with his grandparents reminded me so much of mine, it was hard not to identify with that even though his Cherokee culture was of course different. Still, the love, the knowledge, the ways shown to live were in many ways, different but the same. So Little Tree learns from his grandparents the way of the Indian and how to navigate the world of the white man. Loved watching him learn, change and grow. Of course there is sadness, the white world trying to encroach on the Indian ways, but also he knows he is loved.<br /><br />I knew nothing about this author when I read this book. Only after reading and reading other reviews did I learn about this author's shaded and terrible past. So does one judge this book by its author? I usually think that learning about an author often adds nuances to their books, that are usually passed over. Since an author puts his heart and soul into their stories, there is always something there identifiably the authors own, whether opinions, or memories. So...... Can people change? Going by this book I can't believe the same man who wrote this book was the same man as that of his earlier years. So I was conflicted and decided to judge this book on its own merits rather than judging the authors life. Others may not feel so but that is up to them. I enjoyed this book, enjoyed the story so that is how I will rate it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10794151599111292029" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_80493033" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/80493033" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/80493033?book_show_action=true">Dec 09, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lawyer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lawyer" href="/user/show/3025547-lawyer">Lawyer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/13141867-the-education-of-little-tree">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those interested in how to live.  And why. </span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Lawyer by: </span>
      My well read friend, Lawyer Ritchie Tipton
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=alabama-authors">alabama-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=love">love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=group-read">group-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=on-the-southern-literary-trail">on-the-southern-literary-trail</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=nature">nature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=cherokee-indians">cherokee-indians</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=asa-carter">asa-carter</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=forrest-carter">forrest-carter</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer80493033" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4159088482690554977"><b>The Education of Little Tree: Which is Right</b><br/><br/><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116236.The_Education_of_Little_Tree' title='The Education of Little Tree by Forrest Carter' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Education of Little Tree</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/67388.Forrest_Carter' title='Forrest Carter' rel='nofollow noopener'>Forrest Carter</a> was chosen as the Pre-1980 Group Read by members of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/63470-on-the-southern-literary-trail' rel='nofollow noopener'>On the Southern Literary Trail</a> for June, 2016. Special thanks to Trail Member <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/14260681-tina' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tina</a> for nominating this work.</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br/><br/><blockquote> <a href='http://s1151.photobucket.com/user/mmcsullivan/media/Little%20Tree%20First%20Edition_zpsslx108u8.jpg.html' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1465524233i/19368743.jpg' alt=' photo Little Tree First Edition_zpsslx108u8.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/></a><br/><i>The Education of Little Tree, First Edition, Delacorte Press, New York, New York, 1976</i>
</blockquote><br/><br/><blockquote>
  <a href='http://s1151.photobucket.com/user/mmcsullivan/media/Forrest%20Carter_zpsdl8rynkw.jpg.html' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>
    <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1465524233i/19368744.jpg' alt=' photo Forrest Carter_zpsdl8rynkw.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
  </a>
  <br/>
  <i>Forrest Carter, 1975</i>
</blockquote><br/>This is my third read of this book. It means much to me. For it speaks of the love shared by a young boy and hi</span>
  <span id="freeText4159088482690554977" style="display:none"><b>The Education of Little Tree: Which is Right</b><br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116236.The_Education_of_Little_Tree" title="The Education of Little Tree by Forrest Carter" rel="nofollow noopener">The Education of Little Tree</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/67388.Forrest_Carter" title="Forrest Carter" rel="nofollow noopener">Forrest Carter</a> was chosen as the Pre-1980 Group Read by members of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/63470-on-the-southern-literary-trail" rel="nofollow noopener">On the Southern Literary Trail</a> for June, 2016. Special thanks to Trail Member <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/14260681-tina" rel="nofollow noopener">Tina</a> for nominating this work.</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br><br><blockquote> <a href="http://s1151.photobucket.com/user/mmcsullivan/media/Little%20Tree%20First%20Edition_zpsslx108u8.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1465524233i/19368743.jpg" alt=" photo Little Tree First Edition_zpsslx108u8.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></a><br><i>The Education of Little Tree, First Edition, Delacorte Press, New York, New York, 1976</i>
</blockquote><br><br><blockquote>
  <a href="http://s1151.photobucket.com/user/mmcsullivan/media/Forrest%20Carter_zpsdl8rynkw.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1465524233i/19368744.jpg" alt=" photo Forrest Carter_zpsdl8rynkw.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  </a>
  <br>
  <i>Forrest Carter, 1975</i>
</blockquote><br>This is my third read of this book. It means much to me. For it speaks of the love shared by a young boy and his grandparents. Orphaned at five, Little Tree, a Cherokee Indian, is taken into the home of Granma and Granpa.<br><br>My Mother married young. On a dare, no less. Crossing the Mississippi state line where it was possible to marry at a younger age without parental consent. My father decided he was much too young to be one, though I guess he enjoyed making me. When he abandoned my mother and me I was a week old. We were taken in by my mother's parents.<br><br>I was raised in my Grandparents' home. My Mother completed her growing up in that home. Although I came to excel academically throughout my years in school, without doubt, my most valuable education did not come from text books but my Grandparents, especially my Grandfather, who was always Papa to me.<br><br>I recognize much of this book as the truth. It is a beautiful and wondrous truth. I share much in common with Little Tree. The lessons he was taught by his Grandparents are tenets for a more full and complete life. Living in harmony with the environment. Take only what you need. To take more is only greed. Tolerance for those different than us. Living simply, recognizing the difference between needs and wants. Accepting your self worth, though you may be looked down upon by others who consider themselves higher than you by their perception of social stature, the value of the roots of the history of your people or family. The acceptance of the passing of all things. This is the nature of life. Embrace these truths and live fully, or live in anxiety and stuggle in futility. Live in despair and desparation. I was taught these same truths.<br><br>When this little book was first published, it attracted little attention, little acclaim, no fanfare. It was not until the University of New Mexico issued a paperback edition of the book in 1980 that <i>The Education of Little Tree</i> became a publishing phenomenon. The book was introduced by a Cherokee Native American whose ancestors had been moved from their homes during the infamous Trail of Tears. Forrest Carter had written the book as his autobiographical memoir. He billed himself as a Storyteller to the Cherokee Nation. It is frequently on the reading curriculum of many high schools. Copies have sold in the millions.<br><br><b>Who is Forrest Carter?</b><br><br>In 1975 a darkly tanned man with a mustache walked into an Abilene, Texas, bookstore owned by Chuck and Betty Weeth. He introduced himself as Forrest Carter. He had written his first book, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1433725.Gone_to_Texas" title="Gone to Texas by Benjamin Franklin Carter" rel="nofollow noopener">Gone to Texas</a> under the name <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3349341.Benjamin_Franklin_Carter" title="Benjamin Franklin Carter" rel="nofollow noopener">Benjamin Franklin Carter</a>. That book was reprinted under the title <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4858802.The_Outlaw_Josey_Wales" title="The Outlaw Josey Wales by Forrest Carter" rel="nofollow noopener">The Outlaw Josey Wales</a> under the name <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/67388.Forrest_Carter" title="Forrest Carter" rel="nofollow noopener">Forrest Carter</a>. Clint Eastwood bought the film rights. Carter was doing well. He became an Abilene, Texas, fixture and was a regular dinner guest at the Shipps. It was there Carter began telling his story of being raised as a Cherokee orphan by his grandparents in Tennessee and he was writing his biography.<br><br>But Forrest Carter had a past. He wasn't a Cherokee. He wasn't from Tennessee. He was Asa Carter, born in 1925 in Anniston, Alabama. During the 1950s he was a member of the Ku Klux Klan and founder of a white supremacy group. He formed a splinter group of the KKK which was responsible for an attack on Nat King Cole at a concert in Birmingham, Alabama, in 1956. Carter worked at Birmingham radio station WILD where he broadcast right wing programs supporting anti-semitism and blatant segregation.<br><br><blockquote>
  <a href="http://s1151.photobucket.com/user/mmcsullivan/media/asacarter-header_zpshuyil9pw.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1465525064i/19368852.jpg" alt=" photo asacarter-header_zpshuyil9pw.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  </a>
  <br>
  <i>Asa Carter, Speech Writer</i>
</blockquote><br><br>Carter became a speech writer for Governor George Wallace in the 1960s, penning the vitriolic first Inauguration Speech containing the infamous line, "Segregation now, segregation forever." Carter continued to work through the administration of Governor Lurleen Wallace, who ran in her husband's stead when he could not run for a successive third term.<br><br>However, Carter and George Wallace had a parting of the ways. When Wallace ran for a third term as Governor in 1970 and was elected again, Wallace pushed Carter to the side. Wallace had toned down his segregationist rhetoric. He saw Carter as an extremist. Wallace had no more use for him. On the day of Wallace's third Inauguration, Alabama journalist and author <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/171585.Wayne_Greenhaw" title="Wayne Greenhaw" rel="nofollow noopener">Wayne Greenhaw</a> found Carter behind the State Capital crying. Carter told Greenhaw Wallace had sold out Alabama to the liberals. It was the last time Greenhaw ever saw Carter in person.<br><br>But Greenhaw did see a televised interview between a man who called himself Forrest Carter and Barbara Walters on The Today Show in 1976 talking about his "Autobiography," <i>The Education of Little Tree</i>. Greenhaw recognized the voice and began asking questions of Asa Carter's old associates.<br><br>Greenhaw got a phone call from Carter. "You wouldn't want to hurt old Forrest, would you?" Greenhaw retorted it was all a lie. And he would prove it. Carter hung up. And disappeared once more.<br><br>Forrest Carter <i>was</i> Asa Carter. He died June 7, 1979, of heart failure in Abilene, Texas. He was at work on <i>The Wonderings of Little Tree</i> which was unfinished. He is buried in Anniston, Alabama.<br><br><b>Should This Book Be Read?</b><br><br>This book has been subject to much criticism, most of it based on the personal and political life of Asa Carter. Is this the proper basis for judging a work of literature?<br><br>I say it is not. <br><br>Whatever Asa Carter's actual political beliefs were at one time does not mean he still possessed those beliefs at the time he wrote <i>The Education of Little Tree</i>. His relationship with the Shipps in Abilene, Texas, indicate a completely different person than the man who worked for George Wallace.<br><br>His editor at Delacorte Press, Eleanor Friede, and her husband were Jewish. He was a frequent guest in their home. Carter never uttered a word of intolerance in their presence.<br><br><i>The Education of Little Tree</i> is a work about love and tolerance. The racists in this book are wealthy whites, bureaucrats, politicians, and intolerant preachers. Perhaps Carter portrayed that so well because he knew what it was to hate.<br><br>Native Americans and blacks are respectfully and sympathetically portrayed. For Asa Carter's previous anti-semitic assertions in earlier years, the kindest person in this work aside from Little Tree's Grandparents, is Mr. Wine. A Jew.<br><br>To refuse to read this book because of Asa Carter's previous political life is a form of censorship. I do not believe in censorship in any form. Nor the banning of books. There is far too much of that as it is.<br><br>Do not think I write an apologia for Asa Carter. I detest what he once stood for. However, I am ever mindful of the resilience of human beings and their ability to change. Hatred is a heavy burden to bear. If not exorcised it will destroy the one who carries it. Perhaps Carter wrote this as his penance.<br><br>I believe in the possibility of redemption. As Little Tree would say, "Which is right."<br><br><b>EXTRAS</b><br><br><a href="http://www.thisamericanlife.org/radio-archives/episode/527/transcript" rel="nofollow noopener">Act One. Seeing the Forrest Through the Little Trees.</a> A Transcript from This American Life concerning Forrest/<br>Asa Carter and <i>The Education of Little Tree</i><br><br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="4159088482690554977" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_25154498" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/25154498" />
    <a title="Leah Higginbotham" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1255246-leah-higginbotham"><img alt="Leah Higginbotham" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/25154498?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leah Higginbotham" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leah Higginbotham" href="/user/show/1255246-leah-higginbotham">Leah Higginbotham</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone!</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Leah by: </span>
      Michelle (mother in law)
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer25154498" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15504243808189546135">*Note: there is a lot of controversy and here say about the author of this book. Forget about it and enjoy this book with an innocent mind!<br/><br/>The Education of Little Tree follows a young boy as he follows his Grandpa, learning and loving as he goes. From plowing to whiskey making, it divinely illustrates the power of self. Regardless of external influences, industry, growth, abundance, and love can be grown and cultivated. <br/><br/>This book was so deep and enriching on so many levels. It made me look at my</span>
  <span id="freeText15504243808189546135" style="display:none">*Note: there is a lot of controversy and here say about the author of this book. Forget about it and enjoy this book with an innocent mind!<br /><br />The Education of Little Tree follows a young boy as he follows his Grandpa, learning and loving as he goes. From plowing to whiskey making, it divinely illustrates the power of self. Regardless of external influences, industry, growth, abundance, and love can be grown and cultivated. <br /><br />This book was so deep and enriching on so many levels. It made me look at my own life and what aspects of it were in harmony or out of harmony. Little Tree and his Grandparents lived with the land, not in spite of it like I feel a lot of our population is doing now. It motivated me to plant a good garden, enjoy nature more, love more. <br /><br />One aspect of the book that I really looked deeply at was the small side story of the sharecroppers. They were always going from place to place without ever enough money, food, clothing, etc. They depended heavily on others for their lifestyle. Little Tree and his family lived with the land and met their own needs accordingly, therefore thriving and not left wanting. They lived simply, within their means, and appreciated much. <br /><br />So which of my needs am I meeting through my own means? Naturally, I'm not going to move to a cabin in the woods with no electricity or plumbing. But am I relying too heavily on someone else for my food? My retirement? My happiness? <br /><br />Through my tears upon finishing The Education of Little Tree, I felt gratitude in knowing that true happiness does not come in the form of big houses and fancy cars. I'm working, striving to become more self-reliant, and enjoying the tender moments I have with my sweet family. I plan on making this a regular read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15504243808189546135" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a title="Scott Wojtalik-courter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott Wojtalik-courter" href="/user/show/13925169-scott-wojtalik-courter">Scott Wojtalik-courter</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer645813173" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer7472433122123068059">I remembered enjoying this book when i read it about fifteen years ago. I stuck in on my list of 'have-reads' and gave it high marks. Then I read a little bit about this author. I just am flummoxed, though I shouldn't be; the levels to which people will stoop. Well, you can't deny he was a decent teller of tales, or lies, as Mark Twain might have said. A klansman who formed his own chapter, took part in lynchings, was a political writer who wrote George Wallace's infamous line, 'segregation now,</span>
  <span id="freeText7472433122123068059" style="display:none">I remembered enjoying this book when i read it about fifteen years ago. I stuck in on my list of 'have-reads' and gave it high marks. Then I read a little bit about this author. I just am flummoxed, though I shouldn't be; the levels to which people will stoop. Well, you can't deny he was a decent teller of tales, or lies, as Mark Twain might have said. A klansman who formed his own chapter, took part in lynchings, was a political writer who wrote George Wallace's infamous line, 'segregation now, segregation tomorrow, segregation forever'; this was an evil man who hated the very people to whom this book might have some appeal. I feel somewhat dirty for having enjoyed it, though somewhat hypocritical for not liking it now. Shouldn't art stand on its own? Should I not enjoy Wagner because he was an anti-Semite? Great googley moogley! I'm gonna have to go with erring on the side of my conscience on this one and recant my rating. He misrepresented the story as a true autobiography (though that, in itself, isn't enough; art is largely artifice), and he is not Wagner. This was just a good story, or at least an appealing one, that was made larger by the belief that it was real, and to discover exactly how unreal it was destroys the illusion. And in art, the illusion is everything. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7472433122123068059" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2214243" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2214243" />
    <a title="Sierra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/143255-sierra"><img alt="Sierra" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2214243?book_show_action=true">Jun 21, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sierra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sierra" href="/user/show/143255-sierra">Sierra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2214243" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17050475516039119">I got out of college without reading a heck of a lot of classic literature, American or otherwise. Now I'm trying to make up for lost time. I picked up The Education of Little Tree because there happened to be a copy here at my sister's house. I vaguely remembered there being some controversy á la Rigoberta Menchú or Nick Frey. The reissue I have from 1999 has "AMERICAN INDIANS/FICTION" on the back cover, but the introduction calls it "[Forrest Carter's] autobiographical remembrances of life wit</span>
  <span id="freeText17050475516039119" style="display:none">I got out of college without reading a heck of a lot of classic literature, American or otherwise. Now I'm trying to make up for lost time. I picked up The Education of Little Tree because there happened to be a copy here at my sister's house. I vaguely remembered there being some controversy á la Rigoberta Menchú or Nick Frey. The reissue I have from 1999 has "AMERICAN INDIANS/FICTION" on the back cover, but the introduction calls it "[Forrest Carter's] autobiographical remembrances of life with his Eastern Cherokee Hill country grandparents." I decided to just go ahead and read the book, then google it later.<br /><br />Pre-google review:<br />Touching - check<br />Sage - check<br />Well-written - check<br /><br />The characters were quite endearing and an interesting story unfolds before the backdrop of the Great Depression.<br /><br />Post-google review:<br />You poser!<br /><br />This article in salon.com, entitled "The Education of Little Fraud" slams Forrest Carter (actually Asa Carter), pointing out that he was a founder of the Ku Klux Klan. <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://archive.salon.com/books/feature/2001/12/20/carter/index.html">http://archive.salon.com/books/featur...</a><br />That's a detail that's hard to overlook. Then there's the fact that actual Cherokee Indians have said the book is inaccurate and tends toward the "Noble Savage" take on things.<br /><br />Honestly, where does that leave us?<br /><br />I guess I'm just going to have to call a spade a spade, or, in this case, call fiction fiction and just leave it at that. It's still a good read, taken with a grain of salt. I guess I won't base my entire understanding of the Cherokee way of life on this single 216-page novel. Nor my knowledge of Mayan cosmology on Mel Gibson's "Apocalypto." Now that's a lesson to take home. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17050475516039119" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_41058335" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41058335" />
    <a title="Lynn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1405162-lynn"><img alt="Lynn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1233952030p2/1405162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41058335?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lynn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lynn" href="/user/show/1405162-lynn">Lynn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41058335" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17282160764121057721">I finished this book last weekend. I'd put it up there with The Alchemist and To Kill a Mockingbird, it was that powerful. This is a work of fiction (despite the intro, it is not really an autobiography) about a 5 year old Cheerokee boy who is raised by his grandparents, Cheerokee hill people, after the death of his parents. It is set in depression-era Tennessee. The story is told in vignettes; the chapters in chronological order. It was a quick read, just over 200 pages, with some mild language</span>
  <span id="freeText17282160764121057721" style="display:none">I finished this book last weekend. I'd put it up there with The Alchemist and To Kill a Mockingbird, it was that powerful. This is a work of fiction (despite the intro, it is not really an autobiography) about a 5 year old Cheerokee boy who is raised by his grandparents, Cheerokee hill people, after the death of his parents. It is set in depression-era Tennessee. The story is told in vignettes; the chapters in chronological order. It was a quick read, just over 200 pages, with some mild language. I wish I could describe this book better, but it was so moving and just amazing. <br /><br />I really recommend NOT google-ing the author until after you've read the book, he wasn't a very good person. Although, the story about this book getting the boot from Oprah's list is funny.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17282160764121057721" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1283073338" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1283073338" />
    <a title="Tina " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14260681-tina"><img alt="Tina " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1417033701p2/14260681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1283073338?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tina " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tina " href="/user/show/14260681-tina">Tina </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14260681-tina?shelf=on-the-southern-literary-trail-li">on-the-southern-literary-trail-li</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14260681-tina?shelf=southern-books-to-read">southern-books-to-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14260681-tina?shelf=for-the-kid-in-you">for-the-kid-in-you</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1283073338" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15563385238518888869">"Grandpa said he had many's the time seen that same kind of thing, feelings taking over sense, make as big a fools out of people as it had ol' Rippitt. Which I reckon is so." I think this quote sums up this entire novel. People letting their feelings take over their common sense where others are concerned.<br/><br/>This is a novel about prejudice and discrimination against the Indian, the Jew, the White Man, the Politician, the Christian, the Poor, the Wealthy, the Sinner, the Saint, the Educated and the </span>
  <span id="freeText15563385238518888869" style="display:none">"Grandpa said he had many's the time seen that same kind of thing, feelings taking over sense, make as big a fools out of people as it had ol' Rippitt. Which I reckon is so." I think this quote sums up this entire novel. People letting their feelings take over their common sense where others are concerned.<br /><br />This is a novel about prejudice and discrimination against the Indian, the Jew, the White Man, the Politician, the Christian, the Poor, the Wealthy, the Sinner, the Saint, the Educated and the Uneducated. The Education of Little Tree is told through the voice of a young part Cherokee child and, I feel, it is very timely considering what is going on in our nation today. The lesson here being that prejudice and discrimination comes in many forms and goes in many directions and, most of it, is feelings taking over sense.<br /><br />The irony of this gem is that it was published in 1976 under the pen name for Asa Earl Carter. Mr. Carter was a man whose own history of prejudice and discrimination would make your hair stand on end. I like to think that this book, which was not really autobiographical as Carter touted, was Mr. Carters way of making amends for his past feelings taking over sense. <br /><br />My final rating: 4.5 stars<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15563385238518888869" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_47476436" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/47476436" />
    <a title="Sue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/830760-sue"><img alt="Sue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1550589138p2/830760.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/47476436?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sue" href="/user/show/830760-sue">Sue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780826308795" href="/book/show/829675.The_Education_of_Little_Tree">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/830760-sue?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/830760-sue?shelf=read-2009">read-2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer47476436" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12323104587411159310">This is the story of Little Tree, a five-year-old boy who is brought up by his Cherokee grandparents after his mother dies. Although the introduction claims it's an autobiographical reminiscence, it is in fact fiction. Moreover, the author is not Cherokee; at one point he was apparently a member of extreme racist groups in the USA. <br/><br/>Nevertheless, it's a very well-written book. I gather that some of the details of Little Tree's life and Cherokee customs are not based on reality, but pure fiction; </span>
  <span id="freeText12323104587411159310" style="display:none">This is the story of Little Tree, a five-year-old boy who is brought up by his Cherokee grandparents after his mother dies. Although the introduction claims it's an autobiographical reminiscence, it is in fact fiction. Moreover, the author is not Cherokee; at one point he was apparently a member of extreme racist groups in the USA. <br /><br />Nevertheless, it's a very well-written book. I gather that some of the details of Little Tree's life and Cherokee customs are not based on reality, but pure fiction; that would perhaps upset people from this background, but for me it was a delightful insight into a world I knew nothing about. <br /><br />Moreoever, the book is very pro-Cherokee, and positive about Little Tree's experiences, educational and otherwise. White men are shown to be bigoted and legalistic, and Little Tree's brief foray into a boarding school is heart-breaking. <br /><br />I can only assume that the author had repented of his former beliefs when he wrote it. Some critics consider the language offensive - it's written in a distinctive style, almost as if in five-year-old language at times. But for me, it added to the realism of the story. <br /><br />All in all, I thought it a lovely book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12323104587411159310" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/490528660?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vannessa Anderson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vannessa Anderson" href="/user/show/1380029-vannessa-anderson">Vannessa Anderson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1380029-vannessa-anderson?shelf=favorite-books">favorite-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1380029-vannessa-anderson?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer490528660" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2320451422200045279">The Education of Little Tree touched me on every emotional level!<br/><br/>Little Tree, at age four, went to live with his grandparents after the death of his mom; only a year earlier he’d lost his dad. Little Tree’s grandparents, in their seventies, knew they probably only had a few years to teach Little Tree everything he needed to know to survive on his own started teaching him life’s skills upon their arrival to bring him home to live with them. The story took place in the Appalachian mountains of Ten</span>
  <span id="freeText2320451422200045279" style="display:none">The Education of Little Tree touched me on every emotional level!<br /><br />Little Tree, at age four, went to live with his grandparents after the death of his mom; only a year earlier he’d lost his dad. Little Tree’s grandparents, in their seventies, knew they probably only had a few years to teach Little Tree everything he needed to know to survive on his own started teaching him life’s skills upon their arrival to bring him home to live with them. The story took place in the Appalachian mountains of Tennessee during the Great Depression.<br /><br />While Little Tree was being educated, so was I. Author Carter’s extraordinary imagination and writing skills made me care deeply about Little Tree, his grandparents, and his grandparents friends who stood in courage in spite of the horrendous lifestyle they were force to endure. The Education of Little Tree is on a subject that many would like for us to forget. <br /><br />The Education of Little Tree is filled with history and so much useful information on surviving that you’ll want to write the information down. The Education of Little Tree will serve as a guide to effective parenting. <br /><br />The Education of Little Tree is a book that I will read over and over because with each read, I’ll learn something that I’d missed in a previous reading. <br /><br />The Education of Little Tree was my favorite book of 2012! <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2320451422200045279" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_192552448" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/192552448" />
    <a title="Matthew Moes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5926124-matthew-moes"><img alt="Matthew Moes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1312117973p2/5926124.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/192552448?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Moes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Moes" href="/user/show/5926124-matthew-moes">Matthew Moes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5926124-matthew-moes?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer192552448" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16147753964397026611">Having just finished reading this beautiful story I discovered the controversy surrounding it and its author, only serving to deepen my appreciation for its significance. I found the story beautiful and spiritually moving, making note of several passages that I would like to refer back to from time to time. It is a beautiful story written on the theme of simplicity and natural living. Although it is introduced as an autobiographical account, posterity has unveiled its fictional origins. To me, t</span>
  <span id="freeText16147753964397026611" style="display:none">Having just finished reading this beautiful story I discovered the controversy surrounding it and its author, only serving to deepen my appreciation for its significance. I found the story beautiful and spiritually moving, making note of several passages that I would like to refer back to from time to time. It is a beautiful story written on the theme of simplicity and natural living. Although it is introduced as an autobiographical account, posterity has unveiled its fictional origins. To me, this actually liberates the story from the necessity of authenticity and elevates its moral message to a universal one. This is as opposed to an authentic portrayal of the Cherokee, something I really did not find to be the point in the first place. I would strongly recommend this book for others, young and older, and especially educators, as I found it relevant to reflect on the educational ramifications as we frame the debate today to be between “academic achievement” vs. “human development”.<br /><br /><br /><br />The controversies regarding the author and the so-called stereotypes really make the book even more intriguing in the sense that it would seem the book does indeed serve as the author’s atonement for a life wasted in racist loyalties and personal bigotry. From the author’s biography it would seem that sometime between ending his political career and becoming an author that he really sought to become someone better. In this sense, his fabricated autobiography can be seen as part of this transformation, to the extent of projecting a totally different childhood, value system and worldview other than his own. As others have expressed bafflement at his intentions, I cannot believe that a work as touching and profound as The Education of Little Tree be anything less than sincere.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16147753964397026611" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Natalie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1802409-natalie"><img alt="Natalie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1229285152p2/1802409.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/174951782?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Natalie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Natalie" href="/user/show/1802409-natalie">Natalie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1802409-natalie?shelf=nature-books">nature-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer174951782" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14863183206012620449">A dear new soul recommended me this novel, and am I so GLAD.<br/><br/>It's like Huckleberry Finn meets Laura Ingalls Wilder with Native American culture and folklore thrown in.<br/><br/>Written from the perspective of a little boy (similar to Huck Finn) who's orphaned when his parents die, he's adopted by his grandparents. You learn the Cherokee way of looking at the land, the environment and nature. The intersting thing, is that most novels of this sort take place during the "Wild West" migration period, but this </span>
  <span id="freeText14863183206012620449" style="display:none">A dear new soul recommended me this novel, and am I so GLAD.<br /><br />It's like Huckleberry Finn meets Laura Ingalls Wilder with Native American culture and folklore thrown in.<br /><br />Written from the perspective of a little boy (similar to Huck Finn) who's orphaned when his parents die, he's adopted by his grandparents. You learn the Cherokee way of looking at the land, the environment and nature. The intersting thing, is that most novels of this sort take place during the "Wild West" migration period, but this one doesn't.<br /><br />Interestingly enough, it takes place during the Depression. And through the boy's naivette, we're introduced to some interesting and true to life themes: the forced assimilation of Native Americans to the white man's life (Christianity, mission schools, reservations). <br /><br />Lovingly, the novel never comes across as preachy, which is key when observing the world through a small boy's eyes.<br /><br />I absolutely could NOT stop crying the last three chapters. The only thing I didn't like was that I felt we didn't know enough about where and what Little Tree was going to do now.<br /><br />A must read for elementary teachers to read aloud, mothers and fathers to read to their children, but absolutely lovely for adults, too.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14863183206012620449" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_394782063" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/394782063" />
    <a title="Molly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/101007-molly"><img alt="Molly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349738163p2/101007.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/394782063?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Molly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Molly" href="/user/show/101007-molly">Molly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/7664548-the-education-of-little-tree">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/101007-molly?shelf=ya-and-teen">ya-and-teen</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer394782063" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7257362325486012378">I remember my mother mentioning to me how good this book was said to be when I was a kid in Chattanooga, and then a few years later, how she mentioned the book was written by a former KKK member and was a lie. I also didn't remember this book ever coming up in discussions in creative writing classes about falsehoods and plagiarism and the like, nor do I remember it being mentioned in my American Indian classes.<br/><br/>I held off on reading this book, mostly because I wasn't sure what this knowledge woul</span>
  <span id="freeText7257362325486012378" style="display:none">I remember my mother mentioning to me how good this book was said to be when I was a kid in Chattanooga, and then a few years later, how she mentioned the book was written by a former KKK member and was a lie. I also didn't remember this book ever coming up in discussions in creative writing classes about falsehoods and plagiarism and the like, nor do I remember it being mentioned in my American Indian classes.<br /><br />I held off on reading this book, mostly because I wasn't sure what this knowledge would bring to the text. Are these elements forgivable? It's such a strange story. I watched a documentary on Asa Carter's life and it wasn't clear whether Carter had changed his white supremacist viewpoint, though he had certainly changed his whole life to fit into the fiction of his story--his neighbors and friends were surprised when they learned of his hooded past. He continued to refuse the Asa Earl Carter background, despite rising evidence to the contrary, until his death.<br /><br />The book itself is fine--the voice has some charm, though there is a bit of sappiness and stereotype, particularly that of the 'Noble Savage,' that gave me some discomfort. There are elements that I've always enjoyed reading about--the concept of creating most of one's own things, the library and reading traditions of the family, the intimate observation of the natural world, the importance of family. And I *will* admit to crying at the end (pregnancy hormones?) when the grandmother passes and a note is left for Little Tree that promises they will do it better next time: I thought, as my husband eased our daughter to sleep, aren't we getting it right this time around? I was frightened, I think, of the idea that right here, right now, isn't better, and I was saddened, I know, at how many lives there are out there that aren't right and how lucky we are. To me, if a book can move me to be reminded of those things, I can forgive, just a little, the author's mighty awful past.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7257362325486012378" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1668789162" />
    <a title="Frank" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7620837-frank"><img alt="Frank" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442852163p2/7620837.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1668789162?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Frank" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Frank" href="/user/show/7620837-frank">Frank</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/7664548-the-education-of-little-tree">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7620837-frank?shelf=biography-memoir">biography-memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7620837-frank?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7620837-frank?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7620837-frank?shelf=native-american">native-american</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1668789162" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14780051115666339442">I really enjoyed this book! <i>The Education of Little Tree</i> is the story of a young orphaned 5-year old boy called Little Tree, who is taken in by his half-Cherokee Granpa and Cherokee Granma. The story takes place during the Great Depression in the mountains of Tennessee. “Little Tree” learns how to survive in the mountains and how to respect nature. He also learns the ways of the whites, especially the politicians and tax collectors who are trying to put Granpa out of his whiskey business. The bo</span>
  <span id="freeText14780051115666339442" style="display:none">I really enjoyed this book! <i>The Education of Little Tree</i> is the story of a young orphaned 5-year old boy called Little Tree, who is taken in by his half-Cherokee Granpa and Cherokee Granma. The story takes place during the Great Depression in the mountains of Tennessee. “Little Tree” learns how to survive in the mountains and how to respect nature. He also learns the ways of the whites, especially the politicians and tax collectors who are trying to put Granpa out of his whiskey business. The book is full of wisdom and sometimes hilarious anecdotes relating how Granpa keeps his business afloat and scares off would be investors in his business. While Granpa teaches Little Tree the ways of nature and his trade (whiskey making), Granma teaches him how to read and the benefits of an education. Towards the end of the story, Little Tree is taken away from his grandparents and placed in an orphanage to get a better education, but ends up being beaten and abused by the system. <br><br>This was a very inspiring story and many consider this a classic along with <i>Huckleberry Finn</i> and <i>To Kill a Mockingbird</i>. When it was originally published, it was touted as a true memoir of the author. However, it was later shown to be mostly fiction and it was found out that the author was a former member of the KKK and a speechwriter for Alabama Governor George Wallace. See this <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Education_of_Little_Tree" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia article</a> on the book and its author. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1466294444i/19463927.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Not sure how an ex-KKK member could write such a moving and inspiring story but overall, I would still highly recommend this!</span>
  <a data-text-id="14780051115666339442" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13631618352098180060">I don't know how this has slipped under my radar for so long. Little Tree has captured my heart. This was a beautiful and poignant memoir of a Cherokee boy raised by his grandparents in the Depression. The narrator's voice as a young child was so sweet and completely believable and captivating. <br/><br/>I feel like saying too much would be inappropriate for a book like this, since as Little Tree says, "Granpa said if there was less words, there wouldn't be as much trouble in the world."<br/><br/>On L.T.'s 6th birt</span>
  <span id="freeText13631618352098180060" style="display:none">I don't know how this has slipped under my radar for so long. Little Tree has captured my heart. This was a beautiful and poignant memoir of a Cherokee boy raised by his grandparents in the Depression. The narrator's voice as a young child was so sweet and completely believable and captivating. <br /><br />I feel like saying too much would be inappropriate for a book like this, since as Little Tree says, "Granpa said if there was less words, there wouldn't be as much trouble in the world."<br /><br />On L.T.'s 6th birthday: "Granma said I was lucky, and more than likely one in a hundred million. ... I told Granma I was right proud of the whole thing; and right off, I could tell that I wasn't afraid of dark in the hollows anymore. Granpa said I had the uppers on him, being born special and all. He said he wished he had been picked out for such. Granpa said he had always been hampered with a suspicion of being frightened of the dark, and now would total depend on me to lead him about in dark situations. Which I told him I would."</span>

    <a title="David Hilton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5887002-david-hilton"><img alt="David Hilton" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Hilton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Hilton" href="/user/show/5887002-david-hilton">David Hilton</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer710030663" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18388133443296674273"><br/>This is a beautiful little book that follows the story of 5 year old orphan Little Tree who is taken in, loved deeply, and guided thoughtfully by his grandparents in the Cherokee tradition during the Great Depression. It is a nice contrast to Sherman Alexie's "Diary of a Part Time Indian," as it show Native Americans in wholly different setting but with some of the same values.<br/><br/>Carter's writing is elegant in its simplicity. Little Tree's narration is believable and compelling. Each chapter is its</span>
  <span id="freeText18388133443296674273" style="display:none"><br />This is a beautiful little book that follows the story of 5 year old orphan Little Tree who is taken in, loved deeply, and guided thoughtfully by his grandparents in the Cherokee tradition during the Great Depression. It is a nice contrast to Sherman Alexie's "Diary of a Part Time Indian," as it show Native Americans in wholly different setting but with some of the same values.<br /><br />Carter's writing is elegant in its simplicity. Little Tree's narration is believable and compelling. Each chapter is its own story, but the book hangs together effortlessly. The main characters, and some of the important minor ones - are richly painted. I want to meet these people and learn from them.<br /><br />There's a crazy controversy about the author's background. It turns out the book is fictional and there's more to it than that. But, read The Education of Little Tree before digging into this. I'm afraid it could ruin an otherwise magical experience for you.<br /><br />Do read this book. It is special and shouldn't be missed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18388133443296674273" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Jeanette" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11869072-jeanette"><img alt="Jeanette" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1372095557p2/11869072.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeanette" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeanette" href="/user/show/11869072-jeanette">Jeanette</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1617794062" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13879912295941011311">This is a children's tale (he is the narrator) of the years he spends with his Grandpa and Grandma after his parents have passed. It is sweet, heart-warming and details his Cherokee education of nature and lifestyle. The hounds they own are also full characters and their immersion into events delightful, IMHO.<br/><br/>There are aspects that support stereotype to the max, and yet it is a good read. I enjoyed it. Regardless of the hype and history re the author or his agenda, or its being structured into a</span>
  <span id="freeText13879912295941011311" style="display:none">This is a children's tale (he is the narrator) of the years he spends with his Grandpa and Grandma after his parents have passed. It is sweet, heart-warming and details his Cherokee education of nature and lifestyle. The hounds they own are also full characters and their immersion into events delightful, IMHO.<br /><br />There are aspects that support stereotype to the max, and yet it is a good read. I enjoyed it. Regardless of the hype and history re the author or his agenda, or its being structured into a sentimental flow, I thought it was well worth the read and a loving embrace to Cherokee "eyes" for the surrounds of mountain Tennessee during the Depression years prior to WWII. Be they a rigid translation of the actual truth or an emotional bonding fiction/ rendition.<br /><br />I only lacked giving it that 4th star because for me it "tried too hard" within simplistic categorizing in several scenarios and within some characters and events which Little Tree and his Grandfather witness and effect. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13879912295941011311" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
