<div id="review_2654932188" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2654932188" />
    <a title="Kaan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/91568362-kaan"><img alt="Kaan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1562009623p2/91568362.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2654932188?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kaan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kaan" href="/user/show/91568362-kaan">Kaan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/91568362-kaan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2654932188" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10947285749486076677">The Education of Little Tree is one of those books that captivates its readers with affectionate characters and sophisticated life lessons.<br/><br/><br/><br/>Fun fact: this was not the kind of book I stumbled upon while I was browsing the options at my local bookstore. It was actually recommended by my Turkish teacher at the academy, who wanted us to read three novels for the next book exam. (The other two being Jonathan Livingston Seagull and The Little Black Fish, which are also wonderful books in their own righ</span>
  <span id="freeText10947285749486076677" style="display:none">The Education of Little Tree is one of those books that captivates its readers with affectionate characters and sophisticated life lessons.<br /><br /><br /><br />Fun fact: this was not the kind of book I stumbled upon while I was browsing the options at my local bookstore. It was actually recommended by my Turkish teacher at the academy, who wanted us to read three novels for the next book exam. (The other two being Jonathan Livingston Seagull and The Little Black Fish, which are also wonderful books in their own right.)<br /><br /><br />So I picked all three books when I had the chance, and The Education of Little Tree was the second one I read, the first one being Jonathan Livingston Seagull, however, there's no doubt that the second book was the one which mesmerized me the most. I got so absorbed in its characters, world and inspiring views of life.<br /><br /><br />Written by Forrest Carter and originally published in 1976, the book gives a strong tale of clashing cultures. It tells the story of a young boy whose parents die at an early age. His Cherokee grandmother and half-Cherokee grandfather take him back to their home in the Appalachian mountains of Tennessee. During the Great Depression, and with compassion and humor, his grandparents teach him The Way of the Cherokee. His church-going, hunting, and learning how to make whiskey despite prohibition gives a clear picture of what the world was like for country folk in this time period, but the added detail of the Cherokee culture breathes a sort of life into the setting that I’ve never encountered before.<br /><br /><br /><br />Forrest Carter implants his love for the wilderness in every chapter. I was touched by the delicate way their grandparents introduced him to the world, as their people perceived it. No matter what happens, the family remains strong and proud of their Cherokee ancestry, even though they are the only Indians left in the area. (Aside from Willow John, who is a fantastic character introduced later on in the book.)<br /><br /><br /><br />One of the things that fascinated me about the book was the voice though. The simple yet fluent storytelling from our young protagonist presents us with the touching, charming and yet ironic social and racial commentary which would go over a child's head. As Little Tree gets to know the world around him, the reader also experiences the events alongside him. <br /><br /><br /><br />Even though there are some heartbreaking moments in the book, it's still a refreshing read for those who are looking for the candidness and youthful energy of books such as Heidi, The Little Prince and My Sweet Orange Tree. I recommend you to buy this book and tag along our Little Tree to experience the simple yet beautiful nature, whiskeys and a through and through analysis and reminiscence of the Great Depression era.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10947285749486076677" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_953629539" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/953629539" />
    <a title="Jordan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14463368-jordan"><img alt="Jordan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1352606159p2/14463368.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/953629539?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jordan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jordan" href="/user/show/14463368-jordan">Jordan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer953629539" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2680272125054642133">I don't know what to make of this book. <br/><br/>On the one hand, the book is an endearing "memoir" of a Cherokee life nearly crushed by white hegemony. After being orphaned by his mother's death, and with no father (a point of contention for every white "Christian"), Little Tree lives with his Cherokee grandmother and Scottish-Cherokee grandfather. <br/><br/>Under their loving gaze, Little Tree has simple, anecdotal encounters with white culture, which leave him with a vague sense that he is oppressed by their id</span>
  <span id="freeText2680272125054642133" style="display:none">I don't know what to make of this book. <br /><br />On the one hand, the book is an endearing "memoir" of a Cherokee life nearly crushed by white hegemony. After being orphaned by his mother's death, and with no father (a point of contention for every white "Christian"), Little Tree lives with his Cherokee grandmother and Scottish-Cherokee grandfather. <br /><br />Under their loving gaze, Little Tree has simple, anecdotal encounters with white culture, which leave him with a vague sense that he is oppressed by their ideas:<br />-He sees how Granpa makes whiskey outside the law, but without the means or <i>land</i> to make a living legally. <br />-He sees how his gifts are ill-received by white families, even those desperately in need. <br />-He learns about the Civil War, and how Granpa's kindness toward the Confederacy led to no discernible gains. <br />-And most heartbreakingly, he sees how Christianity and white government are predisposed to separate, subjugate, and punish Little Tree and his family just for existing. <br /><br />For all intents and purposes, this seems like a moving book, that would be accessible for my eighth-grade students. <br /><br />But it was also written by a Klansman. <br /><br />Not just any Klansman, but a fierce segregationist, politician, speechwriter, and KKK leader. <br /><br />Unmitigated author-analysis criticism changes the entire meaning of this book. Does Carter intend for the "education" of Little Tree to refer to his growing awareness of white oppression (as I had seen it), or does he mean Little Tree's vague notion that he is lesser, unrefined, "savage"? These thoughts permeate the book, and understandably so... but are they the "education"? <br /><br />Furthermore, is it still a "memoir" of Carter's life, or some contrived appropriation of Native American culture, created with murky intentions? <br /><br />There are so many unanswered questions about this book, I'm going to run out of characters if I list them all in this review. <br /><br />Considering that my host instructor has chosen this book for our students, and is aware of its origins, I am going to trust that the book is somehow meant as a reparation for Carter's life of hatred. Or at least, that it is not outright racist, but can be interpreted as an affirmation of the legitimacy of Cherokee culture against all odds. Overall, the book gives that impression. <br /><br />But is that what Carter wants me to see? <br /><br /><a href="http://www.powells.com/biblio/1-9780826328090-9" rel="nofollow noopener">Buy this title, mindfully, from Powell's Books.</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2680272125054642133" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_343372048" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/343372048" />
    <a title="Christina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6410898-christina"><img alt="Christina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583329881p2/6410898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/343372048?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christina" href="/user/show/6410898-christina">Christina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6410898-christina?shelf=memoirs">memoirs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6410898-christina?shelf=humour">humour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6410898-christina?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6410898-christina?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6410898-christina?shelf=children">children</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6410898-christina?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6410898-christina?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer343372048" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14171793477331804881">Forrest Carter weaves a story of his young childhood being raised by his Cherokee Grandparents in the Appalachian Mountains during the 1930’s depression era. Carter moves the reader in the spiritual elements of how Little Tree is taught to live with Mother Earth and see the signs that she is rebirthing, he also learns how to plant by the signs of the moon, and listen to the birds call. He also is able to speak to trees and observe Mother Nature’s cycles and all life that she holds. Little Tree a</span>
  <span id="freeText14171793477331804881" style="display:none">Forrest Carter weaves a story of his young childhood being raised by his Cherokee Grandparents in the Appalachian Mountains during the 1930’s depression era. Carter moves the reader in the spiritual elements of how Little Tree is taught to live with Mother Earth and see the signs that she is rebirthing, he also learns how to plant by the signs of the moon, and listen to the birds call. He also is able to speak to trees and observe Mother Nature’s cycles and all life that she holds. Little Tree also learns the Cherokee lessons in dealing with people with respect. Lessons he learns through the Cherokee ways is that one teaches a person how to do something because if you constantly give to a person that person will depend on you and not able to stand on their own two feet and what happens we have taken away that person’s dignity. Carter shows elements of Mark Twain’s writing style and I can easily see Huckleberry Finn and Tom Sawyer striking up a ‘kinship’with Little Tree. This book was at times humorous, heart wrenching, awe-inspiring, and delightful read that no one should miss. <br /><br />However, being suspicious how a child of one so young could recall profound details and inner wisdom spirituality that I had to Google if this story was legit. Unfortunately, Forrest Carter is really Asa Carter whom was a member of the KKK and wrote for George Wallace and is known for "Segregation now! Segregation tomorrow! Segregation forever!"With this knowledge I found it difficult to wrap my mind around how such a man who wrote a beautiful story was also a white supremacist racist? Furthermore, I was dishearten to find out the story was also fictional and not non-fictional and found it disturbing that Carter used a culture to exploit his own book and career, that capture a nation with his story that was truly a sham. Then I came to terms with this and realize everyone has good in them. Asa Carter has a gift that came from God, a gift to weave stories that can move a person’s heart. Therefore, though I do not believe in prejudice or discrimination for any reason, creed, race, colour of skin, gender, or sexual preferences, I still will promote this book and give it 4 out of 5 stars! This story was Popping WOW no matter what circumstances it was written under!!! Please Pick up a Copy and read it!!!<br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="14171793477331804881" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1302803082" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1302803082" />
    <a title="Glen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37469192-glen"><img alt="Glen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530295773p2/37469192.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1302803082?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glen" href="/user/show/37469192-glen">Glen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37469192-glen?shelf=discussion-worthy">discussion-worthy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1302803082" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16267137584421886178">The affected speech pattern of the narrator made the first 100 pages of Little Tree difficult to read. But it picked up by the end and included many touching moments; sometimes profoundly sad, other times hopeful.<br/><br/>I had some doubts about the accuracy of the botanical details and even doubted whether it was autobiographical, suspecting it was a collection of folklore. But I never suspected the truth about this book or its author.<br/><br/>The Education of Little Tree presents itself as the autobiography of </span>
  <span id="freeText16267137584421886178" style="display:none">The affected speech pattern of the narrator made the first 100 pages of Little Tree difficult to read. But it picked up by the end and included many touching moments; sometimes profoundly sad, other times hopeful.<br /><br />I had some doubts about the accuracy of the botanical details and even doubted whether it was autobiographical, suspecting it was a collection of folklore. But I never suspected the truth about this book or its author.<br /><br />The Education of Little Tree presents itself as the autobiography of a half-Cherokee moonshiner boy, mistreated by white "politicians" and clergy. It is actually a work of pure fiction by the former white supremacist, anti-semitic politician, and KKK leader Asa Earl Carter, who allegedly wrote George Wallace's famous line "Segregation now, segregation tomorrow, segregation forever." Source: Wikipedia:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Education_of_Little_Tree">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Educ...</a><br /><br />Asa "Forrest" Carter is dead now, so proceeds from this book cannot benefit him any more.<br /><br />When I found out who wrote it, I became instantly obsessed with the psychological state of the author. I am not the only one to find it so interesting: it is the subject of the <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=5xZ_5kPli7A" rel="nofollow noopener">2011 documentary, "The Reconstruction of Asa Carter"</a> and an even more thought-provoking <a href="http://www.thisamericanlife.org/radio-archives/episode/527/180-degrees?act=1#play" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">This American Life episode, "180 Degrees"</a>.<br /><br />Though written by a former bigot, this book is an examination of bigotry from the standpoint of the oppressed. I completely sympathized with the plight of the main characters and chafed at their sometimes stoic acceptance of their secondary roles in society. I believe that this is the intended effect. I could see this book serving as a bridge for white supremacists to gain a view from the other side of racial tensions and change their opinions.<br /><br />A similar book that felt like a more accurate/honest account of Native American life, persecution, and spiritual beliefs, is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/377580.Seven_Arrows" title="Seven Arrows by Hyemeyohsts Storm" rel="nofollow noopener">Seven Arrows</a>. I suspect that one is "real," but I just don't know any more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16267137584421886178" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('dKyoNI5amPJp99qc+uRSjnyA5F9ddaSw3i8kxNgyj4axL4QSR1WUCav7t0GCNmvUpxZZ0DozdomzF98LfY/f/g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  }

Het vertelt over een jonge vrouw die voorbestemd is de bruid van de koning van Ulster te worden, maar die het land ontvlucht met de minnaar die ze zelf gekozen heeft. De jaloezie van de koning lokt verraad en wraak uit, wat leidt tot de noodlottige afloop die al bij Deirdres geboorte was voorspeld. Roland Holst geeft in deze poetische vertelling een sterk beeld van zijn hoofdpersonen, tegen de achtergrond van eenzame, ruige landschappen. Er staan onvergetelijke passages in Deirdre en de zonen van Usnach, zoals de beschrijving van aanstormende strijdwagens, van het harpspel in de koningsburcht voor de strijd losbarst, of van Deirdres       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/67388.Forrest_Carter">About Forrest Carter</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/67388.Forrest_Carter"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1358979596p3/67388.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/67388.Forrest_Carter">Forrest Carter
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
116
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11004297763391646423">Asa Earl "Forrest" Carter was an American political speechwriter and author. He was most notable for publishing novels and a best-selling, award-winning memoir under the name Forrest Carter, an identity as a Native American Cherokee. In 1976, following the publication success of his western <i>The Rebel Outlaw: Josey Wales</i>, <i>The New York Times</i> revealed Forrest Carter to be Southerner Asa Earl Carter. </span>
  <span id="freeText11004297763391646423" style="display:none">Asa Earl "Forrest" Carter was an American political speechwriter and author. He was most notable for publishing novels and a best-selling, award-winning memoir under the name Forrest Carter, an identity as a Native American Cherokee. In 1976, following the publication success of his western <i>The Rebel Outlaw: Josey Wales</i>, <i>The New York Times</i> revealed Forrest Carter to be Southerner Asa Earl Carter. His background became national news again in 1991 after his purported memoir, <i>The Education of Little Tree</i>, was re-issued in paperback and topped the Times paperback best-seller lists (both non-fiction and fiction). It also won the American Booksellers Book of the Year (ABBY) award.<br /><br />Prior to his literary career as "Forrest", Carter was politically active for years in Alabama as an opponent to the civil rights movement: he worked as a speechwriter for segregationist Governor George Wallace of Alabama; founded the North Alabama Citizens Council (NACC) and an independent Ku Klux Klan group; and started the pro-segregation monthly titled <i>The Southerner</i>.<br /><br />From <i>Wikipedia</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="11004297763391646423" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

This partial loads on almost every page view.  The associated React component makes
a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.

last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
